### J4 paper on "symbols"

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-29T17:58:38-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
There is a paper for review by J4 called,

  "Symbols for logical operators"

at

     http://www.cobolportal.com/j4/files/03-0145.doc

There are statements about the discussion in comp.lang.cobol that I don't
necessarily think are accurate - but others may.  If you (any CLC reader)
have your own comments on this, you might want to contact the J4 chair at:

    Don.Schricker <at> microfocus.com
```

#### ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-29T16:20:31-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bb64k0$2n5e$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net...
> There is a paper for review by J4 called,
>
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>     Don.Schricker <at> microfocus.com

I've responded separately to Don and the rest of J4 on this subject,
actually in response to someone else's query on the topic.

In the referenced document, Mr. Wagner asserts "After a long discussion on
comp.lang.cobol, the majority (unanimous?) opinion is that these symbols
would be welcomed by all especially younger programmers coming from other
programming cultures ...", and the set of symbols he proposes is "^" for
logical AND, "|" for logical OR, and "!" for logical NOT.

I can see the utility of using symbols >, < and = for relational operators
(I am less convinced about >= and <=, but there's not much I can do about
them -- they were added to the COBOL standard nearly twenty years ago).

But I really don't see that the ability to code relation conditions like "a
| = b ^ c <= d ! e >= f" and "a | = b ! c ^ | d | > e" represents a
significant improvement to the capabilities of the COBOL language, or an
improvement to the clarity of the programmer's intent over what the language
currently provides.

"NOT" and "^" are synonymous in ALGOL.  While I understand that perhaps
ALGOL might not be the "language du jour" for college students, there is a
significant body of folks out there who are expected to be "bilingual" in
ALGOL and COBOL.  Making "^" mean "AND" in COBOL, when it already means
"NOT" in ALGOL, is unlikely to contribute to program reliability in those
environments.

Similarly, "!" is synonymous with "OR" in ALGOL, so having it mean "NOT" in
COBOL might present some issues.

At least some dialects of PL/1 also use "^" for NOT and "!" for OR.

If this proposal were adopted, the situation would be something like this:
"^" would represent "NOT" in ALGOL and PL/1 but "AND" in COBOL; "!" would
represent "OR" in ALGOL but "NOT" in COBOL, and "|" would represent "NOT" in
PL/1 but "OR" in COBOL.

The versions of Pascal with which I am familiar require AND, OR and NOT in
Boolean expressions, though more recent dialects may well have allowed for
single-character symbols in that context.  I don't think the use of English
words for logical operators -- in ALGOL, PL/1 or Pascal -- whether or not
symbols can be used instead -- represents a major deficiency in those
languages, even though they have symbols for arithmetic operators and
comparisons.

All that being said, I also have to note that I haven't seen evidence that
his statement " ... the majority (unanimous?) opinion is that these symbols
would be welcomed by all especially younger programmers coming from other
programming cultures ..." can be demonstrated to be true, either for the
readership of comp.lang.cobol or for the programming community at large.
For the programming community represented by Unisys MCP/AS systems, the
introduction of "^" to mean "AND" in COBOL would present more than a bit of
a problem to the person who's used to the "other programming culture" that
uses that symbol to represent "NOT" (in ALGOL) for decades.  For the same
reason, the introduction of "!" as a synonym for "NOT" in COBOL would
present problems to the person who already uses the symbol as a synonym for
"OR".   Granted, these are not likely *new* programmers, but they do
represent a non-negligible portion of the real-world programmers out there.

In fact, I would go so far as to suggest a high likelihood that the
statement, as written, could be demonstrated to be *false*.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-05-29T18:57:02-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bb66og$8l9$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bb64k0$2n5e$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`

```
The following is what I sent to J4 in response to the note that Chuck sent
out (summarized elsewhere in this thread),

    ***


Unlike Chuck, I think adding a "complete set of symbols" for logical
operator would be a "NICE TO HAVE". I do NOT think it is worth a "lot of
time and resources" (much less contentious debate) - but do think it might
be useful.

I WOULD like to see a future COBOL Standard include support for

<> to mean NOT EQUAL (as originally specified in the CCC JOD)

If I were to "suggest" symbols (from the ISO 8859 "Latin-1" - which I think
is a reasonable assumption), then I would probably go with

^ (X'5E') or � (X'AC') - for NOT

| (X'7C') or � (X'A6') - for OR

I do think, however, there is a "problem" with what you use for AND. It
seems to me that the most "logical" would (of course) be "&" - but I believe
that would be impossible to use as BOTH a logical operator and (as currently
defined) the concatenation operator - because of code such as:

If A not = "B" & "C"

(is it a concatenation operator or is this a combined abbreviated
condition?)

If there were GENERAL consensus that adding a complete set of logical
operators represented as SYMBOLS was a GOOD IDEA, then we could discuss what
would be a possible symbol for "AND" (from within 8859-1). "@" (X'3E') might
be a possibility. Others might be "#" "!" "�" or "�" - none of these are
really good, but are possible. (I would be interested in knowing if any of
this "plain text" note causes problems to the non-US recipients)
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>
>"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
>currently provides.

I do. COBOL gives us symbols for relational operators but no symbols for logical
operators. The notational system is asymmetrical. I prefer one system over two,
which is why I prefer GREATER over >. If we could write everything in symbols,
I'd use symbolic notation. 

>"NOT" and "^" are synonymous in ALGOL.  While I understand that perhaps
>ALGOL might not be the "language du jour" for college students, there is a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>environments.

I chose ^ because it looks like the mathematical symbol for AND. & was already
taken by concatenation. The other two are the symbols used in C and Java, which
are much more well-known than Algol and PL/I. If you don't believe me, ask any
recent college grad whether he's _heard_ of Algol, much less learned its
grammar. Then ask whether he's heard of Java. 

>If this proposal were adopted, the situation would be something like this:
>"^" would represent "NOT" in ALGOL and PL/1 but "AND" in COBOL; "!" would
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>comparisons.

You are citing languages designed in the 70s and obsolete in the 80s to 90s.
Nobody programs in ALGOL or PL/I today. Pascal *may* still be used in
classrooms, but not in production code. C++ and Java, on the other hand, have
been the dominant languages for ten years. . 

>All that being said, I also have to note that I haven't seen evidence that
>his statement " ... the majority (unanimous?) opinion is that these symbols
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>represent a non-negligible portion of the real-world programmers out there.

Do you have any evidence to support current development under ALGOL?

>In fact, I would go so far as to suggest a high likelihood that the
>statement, as written, could be demonstrated to be *false*.

It is facile to 'suggest', more challenging to substantiate.
```

```
I was looking through some boxes of old books a while back & I found an 
interesting one which I studied years ago- Joan Hughes: PL/1 Structured
Programming. I've never actually used PL/1 on the job, but when I went to
college it was commonly used for instruction in programming, along with PASCAL.
(This was over 25 years ago.) We also studied books & papers by Wirth, Djistra,
the Yourdan Press series, etc. Paging through this book I thought of how much
valuable information is contained in these old books on programming that people
today are missing. And the languages- PL/1, ALGOL, PASCAL, etc- are still used
as you say.
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ed6b0bf.16033634@news.optonline.net...

> Nobody programs in ALGOL or PL/I today.

Don't know many PL/1 programmers, I admit, but how much do you know about
Unisys MCP/AS system software and the more sophisticated user application
conventions on that system?

ALGOL is virtually universal in that environment.  Combinations of ALGOL and
COBOL are very common, and a great many of our users are expected to be
familiar with both.  As it happens, WFL (a language that serves a purpose on
MCP/AS systems similar to that provided by JCL on IBM large systems) does
not use symbols for logical operators (providing AND, OR, NOT, EQV and IMP).

> Pascal *may* still be used in classrooms, but not in production code.

Much of the MCP/AS intersystem communication software is written in Pascal,
as, I believe, is some of the imaging support software for financial
applications.  Our COBOL85, C and Pascal83 compilers are written in Pascal
compiler is written in Pascal as well.  And I'm very much surprised to learn
that none of these products are used in production.

> C++ and Java, on the other hand, have been the dominant languages for ten
years. .

> >All that being said, I also have to note that I haven't seen evidence
that
> >his statement " ... the majority (unanimous?) opinion is that these
symbols
> >would be welcomed by all especially younger programmers coming from other

> Do you have any evidence to support current development under ALGOL?

Absolutely.

> It is facile to 'suggest', more challenging to substantiate.

The statement in question is this:  "the majority (unanimous?) opinion is
that these symbols would be welcomed by all."   I don't see this as having
been expressed by a majority of the active participants in this forum.  I
don't agree that the use of "^" for AND in COBOL would be welcomed by all.
You assert the truth of the statement I cited. Certainly the idea that the
opinion that the use of "^" for ALL in COBOL would be "welcomed by all" is a
*unanimous* one is rejected by a single disagreement.  Whether this is a
*majority* opinion, as you state, can only be determined through a search of
the archives or a poll of the readership in which everyone is *required* to
participate, and even at that I am as yet unconvinced that comp.lang.cobol
accurately reflects the COBOL user community as a whole.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
Robert Wagner wrote:
> This began with me arguing that words are better than symbols. As expected, most
> took the opposite side while a few, such as Howard, remained neutral. We even
> had a visit from jallan, who posted several long, scholarly articles in support
> of symbols. NObody argued that words are better. 

You know, I'm starting to think that words aren't too bad, if not 
preferable.  :)  I especially think they're more clear for combined 
abbreviated conditions (although I see and understand your opposition to 
the pseudo "OR" in >= and <=).  IOW,

If X > 5 And < 10

vs.

If X is Less Than 5 And Greater Than 10

Maybe I just like typing...  I -do- find it easier to read, though.  I 
guess it's one of those things you don't notice until you try it.  Of 
course, I still like consistency - don't use words in one place and 
symbols in another...  :)

> Now that I'm converted (saved?), I'm STILL on the wrong side. Uhhh .. nobody
> asked for our opinion. Gimme a break. 

:)  I have a feeling that you could say "The sky is blue", and here's 
what you'd get...

DD - "Could you cite evidence on how you came to the conclusion that the 
sky is blue?"

RP - "You only took a sample of 1 sky.  Even if you took a sample of 3 
skies, that's still not enough."

HB - "It's actually 'The sky is blue.'"  ;)
                                     ^
WMK - "Robert, are you ACTUALLY saying that the sky is BLUE?  The HTML 
safety palette for blue does not look anything like the sky - perhaps 
you should say the sky is #7777FF."

PECD - "You haven't seen blue 'til you've seen these great NZ skies..."

I'm not taking sides on any of this.  From what I've seen in my couple 
of months here, you seem to rub a lot of people the wrong way.  (There's 
been lots of traffic on that too.)  I'll admit, when I read the J4 
paper, I was surprised at the use of "unanimous" as well - I didn't read 
it as "there was unanimous agreement that symbols were better than 
words," it appeared to me that you were saying "there was unanimous 
agreement that these symbols should be implemented."  You've since 
clarified, and I replied to Don Schricker via e-mail with my take on it. 
  There are also folks around here who I have yet to see proffer any 
positive information on their own, only sitting back and trying to poke 
holes in others' postulates.

It's actually kind of funny, sitting back and watching some of this. 
Yes, that above is probably a little far fetched (and I mean no 
disrespect to any of the people in whose mouths I put fictitious words). 
  But I have noticed that people will argue with you when they normally 
wouldn't, not for the sake of arguing, but for the sake of you.  The 
private conversations we've had have been great, and I'm the type of 
person that believes you can learn something from anyone, no matter how 
much you already know.  For that reason, I've really tried =not= to form 
bad opinions of anyone here - even the ones that I don't agree with.

Saturdays give me lots of time to type...  But alas, it's naptime and 
the kids are fussy.  (And my youngest just walked in here and hit his 
mother with a fly swatter, so it's definitely naptime for him...)  Have 
a great weekend!
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-06-02T10:14:13+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbdtru$g1k$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bb865h$156q$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed8ad55.146250961@news.optonline.net> <3ED8EDE3.50507@Knology.net> <3eda4057.249437229@news.optonline.net>`

```
Robert Wagner wrote:

> No one has produced a single quote saying words are better. People who
> were indifferent didn't argue for keeping the status quo.

The basic premise that you are working on is completely flawed (as has been 
pointed out to you in many ways).

Your premise is that it should be _all_ words or _all_ symbols.  No one 
else supports this view, nor the idea that one has to 'switch gears' going 
from a 'word' to a symbol. The idea that different keys on the keyboard 
represent a 'different paradigm' is so silly that it doesn't even rate.

You have failed to show _any_ non-trivial system that is all symbols in 
spite of attempts to use this as some sort of analogy.  Maths has words for 
log, sine, and many other.  C operators include sizeof.

We all use systems that are mixtures of symbols and words and accept all of 
these as being useful without whining about how other symbols should be 
invented or how using all words would be better.

These systems are everything from maths to road signs.  

Now many have stated that they prefer to use, say, > rather than GREATER.  
You have failed to convince anyone that the use of the word is 'better', 
presumably it was your intent that if it was 'better' then we should be 
using it.  No one tried to make you use '>', the arguments were why the 
words _aren't_ better for them.

The second flaw that is inherent in your arguments is that you extrapoled 
in a completely incompetent way.  You translated:

              I prefer '>' over GREATER.
into:         I prefer symbols over words.

This is yet another example of taking one sample (well actually 3: <=>) and 
expanding it into a generalisation that is completely unwarranted.

You extended your faulty extrapolation one stage further to:

              Therefore I will prefer 'some squiggle' over 'and'.

As has been explained in great detail, and which you actually said you 
understood: Preference is to what one is _used_ to. The actual statement 
used by several was of the form:

       I prefer '>' over 'GREATER' because I am more used to it.

In the same way your preference _may_ be stated as:

       I (RW) prefer GREATER over > because I am more used to it.

(this would explain 'switching gears' to something you are _not_ used to).

You _failed_ to take this into account.  Maybe deliberately, maybe because 
you just don't understand, maybe to continue your crusade to 'rub our faces 
in it', maybe because you have an obsession with some demented form of 
'linguistics' that you use to cover your inferiority complex from years of 
'put downs' by C programmers (and loss of job to them).

The flaw is that no one (or very few) _can_ say:

       I prefer '^' over 'and' because I am more used to it.
   
Not Cobol programmers, not programmers in (almost all) other languages, not 
me, not you, not english speakers.  Many other modern, recent languages 
also use and, or, not rather than symbols.  The designers and users showed 
a preference for these to be expressed as words even though symbols were 
used for the comparisons.

Even if there was _any_ demand for symbols yours were a very poor choice 
because of many reason, mostly that they are used for something 
_completely_different_ and thus confusing, but also because they are not in 
the Cobol set, and the reasons why they are not.

Now you seem to have accepted that you wrong and have changed to suggesting 
C's '&&' and '||'.  This does show how shallow and superficial your 
proposal actually was, you had obviously put little _thought_ into it.  It 
was purely an emotional reaction to your inability to fall into line with 
your poor 'ideas'.

You claim for majority support is wrong and flawed.  You obviously 
extropolated 'I prefer > over GREATER', via incompetant combination with 
your 'all symbols' into 'I prefer 'some squiggle' over 'and''.

It is also obvious that you have no intention to adopt these symbols 
yourself, the proposal may have been an attempt at coersing people into 
using your 'all word' preference.  Perhaps you thought that if people said 
'I don't like those symbols' then they would switch to using all words.

Or perhaps it really was an attempt to make yourself look like a complete 
idiot, because _that_ worked.
```

```
Robert Wagner wrote:

> *My* premise is all words or all symbols for LOGICAL RELATIONSHIPS.

Exactly.  Of course you don't get that when the variables used are words 
and the comparisons are symbols.  I suggest that you stick with all words 
if that will make you happy.  You can also express fixed numeric values as 
words using constants if you wish.

Given that all symbols is impractical unless we have a new mechanism for 
defining variables, then I don't see any point in making any change.

> I don't care about sizeof or log, nor did I ever mention them.

Cobol in having a useful mixture of words and symbols, an _appropriate_ mix 
in my opinion.

> Familiarity is NOT the reason I prefer it.  The reason is consistency.
```

```
linking" transaction programs (which you can't do), and they came back 
with "why not make a subsystem?"  As we talked, they explained that a 
subsystem -can be- (but doesn't have to be) similar to a DLL, where you 
can drop custom subprograms, and as long as the calling sequence doesn't 
change, the main programs don't have to be recompiled.  I'm really 
excited about getting that going...

Before you know it, CAMS is going to have a component library...  :)
```

```
The straw man fallacy is to misrepresent someone else's position so that it
```

```
in J4/03-0145.  Whether the document you wrote accurately represents your
actual position (then *or* now) is irrelevant to that point.

According to the Webster's Ninth Collegiate entry for "straw man":  "a weak
or imaginary opposition (as an argument or adversary) set up only to be
easily confuted.

I have not misrepresented the argument *you* posited in J4/03-0145.  You are
the one that proposed the abbreviations ^ for AND, | for OR and ! for NOT in
COBOL.  You are the one that asserted that the majority, and perhaps even
the entirety, of the COBOL user community supports not only the use of
*some* symbols in this context but the use of *these* symbols.  Those are
the arguments as reflected in the *plain text* of your document.

Whether *your* arguments as written can be easily confuted or not is
irrelevant to that point.  I didn't misstate your document; in fact, I went
to great pains to reflect it accurately.  You may well have *since* changed
some or all of the positions taken in this paper (I see some evidence in
CLC, for example, that if somebody could come up with symbols to be used as
abbreviations for AND, OR and NOT other than those you have proposed you
would not object)

Just so there's no doubt about this, I post herewith the *entirety* of the
discussion from the body of the subject document from the J4 web site:
==========
I suggest adding symbols for the logical operators:



AND  ^

OR   | (pipe symbol)

NOT  !



After a long discussion on comp.lang.cobol, the majority (unanimous?)
opinion is that these symbols would be welcomed by all, especially younger
programmers coming from other (programming) language cultures. Several
bi-lingual programmers have said they find it disconcerting, as a matter of
style, to mentally switch gears for COBOL.



The group also strongly favors '<>'. We understand that's already under
consideration.

==========

Which portions of this message would you modify to make the document a more
accurate reflection of your position?  Which portions of your argument, as
presented above, have I misrepresented?

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3edda21b.471106765@news.optonline.net...

The problem is, Robert, that's not what you wrote.  You had a COLON after
the word "operators", listed the three symbols you suggested, and then
referred to "these symbols".

What you intended "these" to refer to is not what the plain rules of English
require that "these" refer to in what you wrote.

Had you written "I suggest adding symbols for the logical operators, *such
as the following* ...", and written " ... such symbols would be welcomed by
all ..." instead of "... these symbols would be welcomed by all ...", then
yes, I might rightly be accused of misunderstanding your document.

> You took "these" as a reference to the specific symbols I suggested.

As the rules of English require.

> In retrospect, I should have written "The majority (unanimous?) opinion is

> symbols for the relational operators (listed above) would be welcomed by
all."

No, that doesn't quite do it, Robert. The use of "^" as a synonym for AND
would *not* be "welcomed by all".

If what you meant was "some such symbols", then changing "(listed above)" to
"(such as those listed above)" would accomplish that.

Note that the evidence you have thus far provided has not yet convinced me
of the universal truth of your statement that introducing symbols for the
logical operators would be "welcomed by all", "welcomed by the majority" of
the COBOL user base, or even "welcomed by a significant percentage of the
readership of comp.lang.cobol".  Can you provide a bit more in the way of
statistical evidence that supports this blanket assertion?

> Sorry about  the ambiguity.

There's no ambiguity in what you originally wrote; its meaning is crystal
clear to me.  It's what you *intended* to convey with that writing at the
time you wrote it that's to me (now) not yet clear.  In reading any
unambiguous text, I presume it means exactly what it says.  If your ability
to express *exactly* what you meant was lacking, that's not my fault.
Others have noticed a tendency for you to decide after the fact that you
really *meant* something other than what you actually *wrote*; far be it
from me to point out my suspicions that this could be but one more among
myriad such examples.  To post something containing such a misrepresentation
in CLC is one thing; to submit it as a formal document to J4 and/or WG4 is
quite another, I would contend!

     -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-05T08:35:39-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbno0c$2fe$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bb64k0$2n5e$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed6b0bf.16033634@news.optonline.net> <bb865h$156q$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed8ad55.146250961@news.optonline.net> <3ED8EDE3.50507@Knology.net> <bbfu3m$frk$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0a96.366765655@news.optonline.net> <bbif0v$2ae1$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edda21b.471106765@news.optonline.net> <bbl3m9$15vo$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edf08dc.562961355@news.optonline.net>`

```
I wrote:

> >Note that the evidence you have thus far provided has not yet convinced
me
> >of the universal truth of your statement that introducing symbols for the
> >logical operators would be "welcomed by all", "welcomed by the majority"
of
> >the COBOL user base, or even "welcomed by a significant percentage of the
> >readership of comp.lang.cobol".  Can you provide a bit more in the way of
> >statistical evidence that supports this blanket assertion?

Robert wrote:

> My response to Richard:
>
> Before I made the request, NO poster said he didn't want or didn't need
> symbols.Three said they were neutral, the other approx. seven were
strongly in
> favor of symbols. Opposition emerged only AFTER I made the request. Thus
> "majority (unanimous?)" was accurate at the time of writing.

Not a response to my question.

What you wrote can, I believe, accurately be summarized for this discussion
as:

(a) After a long discussion on comp.lang.cobol, the majority (unanimous?)
opinion is that these symbols would be welcomed by all, especiailly younger
programmers ...

Grammatically, that's NOT the same thing as any of the following:

(b)  After a long discussion on comp.lang.cobol, the majority (unanimous?)
opinion of those who participated in the correspondence is that they would
welcome these symbols, especially those younger participants in the
correspondence who are programmers ...

(c)  After a long discussion on comp.lang.cobol, the majority (unanimous?)
opinion of the participants in that forum is that they would welcome these
symbols, especially younger programmers ...

(d)  After a long discussion on comp.lang.cobol, the majority (unanimous?)
opinion of COBOL programmers worldwide is that they would welcome these
symbols, especially younger programmers ...

(e)  After a long discussion on comp.lang.cobol, I have concluded that the
majority (unanimous?) opinion of those who participated in the
correspondence is that they would welcome these symbols, especially those
younger participants in the correspondence who are programmers ...

(f)  After a long discussion on comp.lang.cobol, I have concluded that the
majority (unanimous?) opinion of the participants in that forum is that they
would welcome these symbols, especially younger programmers ...

(g)  After a long discussion on comp.lang.cobol, I have concluded that the
majority (unanimous?) opinion of COBOL programmers worldwide is that they
would welcome these symbols, especially younger programmers ...

Note that by English grammar rules, in examples (b) through (d) your "after"
phrase doesn't qualify or limit in any way who HOLDS the opinion, it weakly
qualifies the VERB in the sentence -- "is" -- and can almost be rendered
parenthetically.    Thus, in (b) the people described as holding the
opinions are those who have expressed the opinion; in (c), the people who
read comp.lang.cobol, whether they post or not; and in (d) the entire COBOL
programming community.

"The opinion", in (a), is the most universal of those sets because the
subject of the sentence is unqualified.  The plain English sense of (a) is
that of (d).  And the reason for that is that "after" doesn't qualify
"opinion", it qualifies "is" (if it qualifies anything).

Example (a) could have been written

(h) The majority (unanimous?) opinion is that these symbols would be
welcomed by all, especiailly younger programmers ... The evidence for this
is the long correspondence in comp.lang.cobol ...

Examples (e) through (g) are far less sweeping; "after" qualifies "have
concluded", and makes clear that what you are describing is a *personal*
conclusion and not a universal truth.

The only one of these examples that *might* be provable to be true is (e),
and I'm not convinced.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
The majority here advocate Free Choice. 'You do it your way; I'll do it my way.
You use words; I prefer symbols. Nobody is trying to convince you his preference
is Bettter.' 

My proposal is in accord with that majority opinion. It gives programmers a
choice between words and symbols for logical operators. Moreover, it is in
accord with the existing COBOL standard, which provides a choice for relational
operators. Whatever argument got relational symbols into the standard should
apply equally to logical operators.
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ee36a57.200127006@news.optonline.net...
> >It does, however have two problems (as already raised - I work on the
> >basis that somwthing said often enough does actually get you to notice
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> J4 members don't need to be instructed on COBOL. Pointing that out would
look
> pedantic.

Actually, Robert, that's the heart of the issue as far as I am concerned.
Is the use of ^ for AND, ! for NOT and | for OR so universally accepted
outside of COBOL that increasing the Basic Special Character list to include
them (regardless of the current composition and origins of that character
set) as to warrant such a change?  I haven't yet been convinced.

> J4 isn't tasked with SQL issues. If you want your programs to be
compatible with
> SQL, use the words we already have. Millions of COBOL programs contain no
ESQL.

J4 is, however, tasked with making COBOL useful to those programmers who
would use it.  I do not see a difference in the way expressions are
constructed between the COBOL part of a COBOL program and any SQL that might
be embedded within it as being conducive to clarity.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bb64k0$2n5e$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed6b0bf.16033634@news.optonline.net> <bb865h$156q$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed8ad55.146250961@news.optonline.net> <3ED8EDE3.50507@Knology.net> <bbfu3m$frk$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0a96.366765655@news.optonline.net> <bbif0v$2ae1$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edda21b.471106765@news.optonline.net> <bbl3m9$15vo$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edf08dc.562961355@news.optonline.net> <bbno0c$2fe$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ee2163d.113048395@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ee2163d.113048395@news.optonline.net...
> The majority here advocate Free Choice. 'You do it your way; I'll do it my
way.
> You use words; I prefer symbols. Nobody is trying to convince you his
preference
> is Bettter.'
>
> My proposal is in accord with that majority opinion. It gives programmers
a
> choice between words and symbols for logical operators.

No.  Proposing additional symbols to the Basic Special Character list of the
COBOL standard would do that, through the use of REPLACE.   And any change
in this area would have to start there, because the symbols in question are
not currently part of that list.

The second part of your proposal, however, is to change the standard so that
the symbols would be interpreted in the specified way *without* the replace.
That *denies* the programmer the ability to choose e the symbols for any
other purpose in that context.

> Moreover, it is in
> accord with the existing COBOL standard, which provides a choice for
relational
> operators. Whatever argument got relational symbols into the standard
should
> apply equally to logical operators.

Why shouldn't the argument to *all six* forms of operators (arithmetic,
boolean, concatenation, invocation, logical and relational)?  By your
argument we should have "words" and "symbols" in all context in which either
can appear, right?  I don't think you've answered that one yet (and no, ADD
isn't an operator, it's a verb, and no, "&" isn't a synonym for the STRING
...)... Any proposal less than that does not successfully argue on
"symmetry" or "elegance" grounds; it's half-baked.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

>
>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:3ee2163d.113048395@news.optonline.net...

>The second part of your proposal, however, is to change the standard so that
>the symbols would be interpreted in the specified way *without* the replace.
>That *denies* the programmer the ability to choose the the symbols for any
>other purpose in that context.

Does the standard say you cannot REPLACE a reserved word? Is this invalid:

REPLACE ==move== by ==add==.

>> Moreover, it is in
>> accord with the existing COBOL standard, which provides a choice for
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>"symmetry" or "elegance" grounds; it's half-baked.

Four out of six offer symbols and 'wordy' ways to accomplish the same thing.
Only logical and boolean do not.

arithmetic

  add 1 to foo
  compute foo = foo + 1

concatination

  01  abc.
   05  value 'ab'   pic xx.
   05  value 'c'     pic x.

  move abc to d
  move 'ab' & 'c' to d

invocation

  invoke identifier literal ...
  identifier::literal(...)

relational

  if a greater than b
  if a > b

boolean and logical

  05 a  pic 1.
  05 b  pic 1.
  05 c  pic 1.

  compute c = a or b
  compute c = a b-or b
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-10T15:26:07-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bc5lu0$m3u$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bb64k0$2n5e$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed6b0bf.16033634@news.optonline.net> <bb865h$156q$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed8ad55.146250961@news.optonline.net> <3ED8EDE3.50507@Knology.net> <bbfu3m$frk$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edc0a96.366765655@news.optonline.net> <bbif0v$2ae1$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edda21b.471106765@news.optonline.net> <bbl3m9$15vo$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3edf08dc.562961355@news.optonline.net> <bbno0c$2fe$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ee2163d.113048395@news.optonline.net> <bc2sf3$1olu$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ee5b201.128968159@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ee5b201.128968159@news.optonline.net...


> Does the standard say you cannot REPLACE a reserved word? Is this invalid:
>
> REPLACE ==move== by ==add==.

No, it doesn't.  Works like a champ.  Reserved words qualify as text-words,
and the pseudo-text contains neither a separator period nor a separator
semicolon.

This COBOL85 program:
   *replace ==move== by ==add==
    identification division.
    data division.
    working-storage section.
    01  a  pic 9 value 1.
    01  b  pic 9 value 2.
    procedure division.
    only-paragraph.  move a to b.  display b.  stop run.
displays the value 1, whereas this program
    replace ==move== by ==add==.
    identification division.
    data division.
    working-storage section.
    01  a  pic 9 value 1.
    01  b  pic 9 value 2.
    procedure division.
    only-paragraph.  move a to b.  display b.  stop run.
displays the value 3, which is what I'd expect.

What's your point?

> Four out of six offer symbols and 'wordy' ways to accomplish the same
thing.

Changing the rules again.  "Wordy ways to accomplish the same thing" is not
what we were talking about.  We were talking about direct symbolic
replacement -- abbreviations.  And the only operators that have
abbreviations are the relational ones.  That makes one out of six of the
sets of operators that have may be expressed equivalently -- synonymously -- 
in all contexts.

> Only logical and boolean do not.

False statement.  You may, in your heart of hearts, really want it to be
true.  But it's not.

> arithmetic
     >
>   add 1 to foo
>   compute foo = foo + 1

Is "add 1 to foo rounded" the same as "compute foo = foo + 1 rounded"?

How about "compute foo = foo add 1 rounded"?

> concatination

Don't know what this is.  Presuming this is an illiteracy for
"concatenation":

>   01  abc.
>    05  value 'ab'   pic xx.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>   move 'ab' & 'c' to d

Hey!! What a revelation!!  Since I can, according to you, write
    STRING abc (1:1) DELIMITED BY SIZE,
                abc (2:1) DELIMITED BY SIZE,
                abc (3:1) DELIMITED BY SIZE
    INTO D
and it mostly produces the same results as both of your examples, there's no
need EVER to use either the concatenation operator OR the MOVE verb, and
STRING, MOVE and & should all be considered synonyms!  Right?

> invocation
>
>   invoke identifier literal ...
>   identifier::literal(...)
>

Both context and functionality differ between the inline invocation OPERATOR
and the explicit INVOKE statement.  You may want them to be equivalent.  But
they're not.

> relational
>
>   if a greater than b
>   if a > b

That one I grant.

>
> boolean and logical
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

How 'bout substituting PIC 9 in the above?

You seem to be laboring under the (truly bizarre) misapprehension that
because there are two ways of accomplishing some specific construct that the
constructs are equivalent.   You may want them to be, but they're not.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
Mr Wagner, it still disproves unanimity of favor; 'apathy' is, literally, 
'without feeling'.  Consider:

A: 'I support this strongly!'

B: 'This doesn't bother me.'

C: 'This doesn't bother me.'

D: 'This doesn't bother me.'

E - Y have opinions but do not voice them

Z: 'The supporting opinion is majority/unanimous!'

In this case it should be apparent that the expressed opinion is, by 
majority count, one of apathy and the unexpressed opinions are 
unquantifiable.
```

```
We could use double symbols for some reserved words, like >> for PERFORM.

Instead of:

PERFORM  0100-PROCESS-TRAN
       THRU  0100-PROCESS-TRAN-EXIT.

WE could use:

>> 0100-PROCESS-TRAN  0100-PROCESS-TRAN-EXIT <<

OR

PERFORM  0100-PROCESS-TRAN
       THRU  0100-PROCESS-TRAN-EXIT.
                   VARYING  I-BUBBL  FROM 2  BY  10
                         UNTIL  I-BUBBL  >  50
                                OR IND-EOF-TRAN

>> 0100-PROCESS-TRAN  0100-PROCESS-TRAN-EXIT <<
(I-BUBBL,2,10,>,50,!,IND-EOF-TRAN)


Only kidding. I think.
```

```
On  9-Jun-2003, "Harley" <dennis.harley@worldnet.att.net> wrote:

> I don't see what all the fuss is about.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Code in shorthand, expand, and compile.

One could write macros to allow one to convert non-code to code all over the
place.   But why?   If you make the real code your native language, you avoid
problems when looking at someone else's code.

My fingers are already on the keyboard, typing in a word is easier than calling
a macro - and it looks right.   We don't have to rely on the expansion not
working correctly - formatting is WYSIWYG, and everything is simple.

I like simple.

(And most everybody these days realizes that the best thing they can do here is
to learn to type well).
```

```
You could assign the macro to a pf key.

I have all sorts of edit macros. (ISPF Rexx).
I have one to convert a PROC to JCL, comes in handy.
If I'm editing a program, and want to see a copy book, it's a few key
strokes away.
I have one that shows the FD's, 01 levels, Divisions, and Paragraphs.
I collapse a program, then do a "find all" to show me where something is
used.

If you want to do a COBOL interview, sit the person down at a terminal, edit
a COBOL program, and see what they do with the text editor.
Chance are, if they can't use the editor, they probably won't be a
productive asset.
```

```
On  9-Jun-2003, "Harley" <dennis.harley@worldnet.att.net> wrote:

> If you want to do a COBOL interview, sit the person down at a terminal, edit
> a COBOL program, and see what they do with the text editor.
> Chance are, if they can't use the editor, they probably won't be a
> productive asset.

I make sure I only change the Fkey settings in a minimalist manner - so that
others can use my keyboard.

I have to change F1 to recall, as that function needs to be available.   I 
changed F2 and F9 to Start and Swap Next, as these work about the way the
defaults do, with more screens.   And in SDSF I have F19 & F20 as PREV and NEXT,
working in a similar way as F7 & F8.   F13-24 are pretty much changeable though,
but since they are another keystroke, I save them for complicated tasks.

Not for expanding out abbreviations.

When someone wants to know how to do something in TSO around here, he goes to
me.  It's no great skill, but I am lazy and found learning the commands to be
less work than not knowing them.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-06-10T19:37:09+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bc2nkv$g61$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <217e491a.0306071658.9d74df@posting.google.com> <bc288l$a9d$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <Pb2Fa.195938$ja4.10382205@bgtnsc05-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
Harley wrote:

> It is very easy to write edit macros to substitute symbols for words, and
> vice versa.

I use an editor that has keystroke macros so that most keywords are 
available as two keystrokes f9-P -> PERFORM, f9-V -> VARYING etc. 

It saves time and keystroke errors and also puts them in my preferred 
'Modula-2' style of upper case keywords with mixed case names. 

I don't see much point in doing this with OR, or even AND and NOT. Using 
symbols to save one or two keystrokes is hardly a great leap in 
productivity.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

- **From:** "Harley" <dennis.harley@worldnet.att.net>

```
"Richard Plinston" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message

and
| > vice versa.

| available as two keystrokes f9-P -> PERFORM, f9-V -> VARYING etc.

|
| It saves time and keystroke errors and also puts them in my preferred
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
| productivity.

It wasn't to save 1 or 2 keystrokes.
It might be handy for things like perform or compute.
I agree with you, it seems silly to me.
One of the things I like about COBOL it that it not C.
When a co-worker suggested I learn C, I got the K&R, took a look, and
decided it wasn't for me.
 I had worked in PL/1 , and imagined the nightmares some of the PL/1
programmers could create in C.

The point I was trying to, and failed, to make is that if someone is more
comfortable reading symbols, they could replace words with symbols.
Depending on the text editor, changing ' and ' (spaces are needed) to ' & '
shouldn't be that difficult.
```

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> This began with me arguing that words are better than symbols. As expected,
> most took the opposite side while a few, such as Howard, remained neutral.
> We even had a visit from jallan, who posted several long, scholarly articles
> in support of symbols. NObody argued that words are better. 

Then you failed to understand what was said.

Actually you didn't, I know that you did understand the real issue,
but you have either forgotten it or ignore it.

The argument is:

   What is 'better' for one is what one is used to.

You are used to words so they are better _for_you_.

I am used to '< > = >= <= NOT = AND OR' in Cobol, so they are better
_for_me_.

I am also used to these in other languages (with minor changes) and
with C/C++ operators and these are better in those languages _for_me_.

No one except you (RW) has expressed any problem with 'switching
paradigms' between words and symbols.

Some have argued that >= is more readily understood (_for_them_) than 
the equivalent NOT <.  This is _NOT_ altered by making it '! <'  it is
the concept of NOT that creates the need to make a reversal, not the
change between words and symbols.

Introducing _new_ symbols, especially ones that mean something
different in other languages, means that _no_one_ is used to them,
especially in that context.

Given that 'better' is what one is used to, then _no_one_ will find
them 'better'.


> Now that I'm converted (saved?), 

No one 'converted' you.  You were advocating that your way was 'best'
in some sort of absolute way.  It may have been better for you but no
one else found words 'greater than' to be better for them except when
using obsolete equipment.

Others only expressed what _they_ found better for themselves, their
preferences.  There was no intent to make you change, just to stop
your attempts at changing us.

Now you are _still_ trying to change us by coersing us with 'symbols'.

> I'm STILL on the wrong side. 

No one wants symbols for and, or, not.  No one wants _those_ symbols.

> Uhhh .. nobody asked for our opinion. 
> Gimme a break.

You never understood why they didn't use your 'better' use of words,
you never understood how they could do transformations without it
being your exact set of rules, now you don't understand why they don't
want your changes.

If you _really_ want to write code using symbols for and, or, not then
please do so and write a pre-processor.  But I doubt that you would
ever actually use them, because its not about symbols, it is all about
RW's ego.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** jallan@smrtytrek.com (jallan)
- **Date:** 2003-06-01T16:56:56-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<299f1138.0306011556.4b3cfa63@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bb64k0$2n5e$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed6b0bf.16033634@news.optonline.net> <bb865h$156q$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <3ed8ad55.146250961@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote in message news:<3ed8ad55.146250961@news.optonline.net>...

> This began with me arguing that words are better than symbols. As expected, most
> took the opposite side while a few, such as Howard, remained neutral. We even
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> asked for our opinion. Gimme a break.

"Even had a visit from jallan"?

Am I famous or something?

Well to speak for myself, yes I would prefer symbols to "AND", "OR",
and "NOT" in COBOL.

I prefer symbols for operators.

There's lots else I find wrong with COBOL also, mostly the same old
things a lot of people find wrong with it. I find things wrong with c
also, and Perl and Xbase and so forth, mostly the same old things most
people find wrong with them.

Replacing "AND", "OR" and "NOT" in COBOL with symbols, while to my
mind a desireable change, is hardly a crusade. I'm used to them in
COBOL and various forms of BASIC and in xBase and so forth and they
are reasonably short.

But if symbols were to be introduced, well with Unicode and all, why
not go with the actual real and true and honest logical symbols that
(golly! gosh!) real logicians use?

For AND use U+2227, the actual LOGICAL AND symbol, allowing /\ as a
fallback for those stuck with ASCII/EBCDIC.

For OR use U+2228, the actual LOGICAL OR symbol, allowing \/ as a
fallback for those stuck with ASCII/EBCDIC.

For NOT use U+00AC, (ï¿½), the actual NOT SIGN which is available in the
standard Latin-1 alphabet and available in EBDCIC ever since there was
an EBCDIC, even on the keyboards(!) of EBCDIC terminals, but allowing
also ~ as a fallback, this being the older NOT symbol used by
logicians and also used in c (for bit-not).

My suggestion to use "/\" and "\/" is not original. According to Bob
Bemer at http://www.bobbemer.com/BACSLASH.HTM:

<< I had called a joint meeting of IBM, SHARE, and GUIDE, to
regularize the IBM 6-bit set to become the standard BCD Interchange
Code [76]. Frequency studies of symbol occurrence had been prepared,
particularly from ALGOL programs. The meeting of 1961 July 6 produced
general agreement on a basic 60-64-character set, which included the
two square brackets and the reverse slant, which was chosen in
conjunction with "/" to yield 2-character representations for the AND
and OR of early ALGOL. This is reflected in the set I proposed to ANSI
X3.2 on 1961 September 18. >>

Maybe at long last the backslash may be used for the purpose for which
it was invented.

But probably not. Using the correct signs would be too simple.


Jim Allan
```

```
>also ~ as a fallback, this being the older NOT symbol used by
>logicians and also used in c (for bit-not).

I concur. May I add that to my proposal?

>My suggestion to use "/\" and "\/" is not original. According to Bob
>Bemer at http://www.bobbemer.com/BACSLASH.HTM:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>But probably not. Using the correct signs would be too simple.
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ed8ad55.146250961@news.optonline.net...
> >> Pascal *may* still be used in classrooms, but not in production code.

I responded:

> >Much of the MCP/AS intersystem communication software is written in
Pascal,
> >as, I believe, is some of the imaging support software for financial
> >applications.  Our COBOL85, C and Pascal83 compilers are written in
Pascal
> >compiler is written in Pascal as well.  And I'm very much surprised to
learn
> >that none of these products are used in production.
>
> Thank you for the substantiation.

Let me clarify for you.  The statement
    >Pascal *may* still be used in classroms, but not in production code.
is patently, and provably, false.   I have cited the examples that prove it
false.

I had thought my comment that I was surprised to learn otherwise would be
taken as obvious sarcasm.  The management and staff involved in MCP/AS
compiler development are completely unaware of the demise of Pascal in any
production environment, me included.  So are the customers who continue to
submit queries, potential bug reports, and requests to new features to our
support organization, both for the compilers themselves and for the Unisys
products that are dependent on them.

The Unisys MCP/AS COBOL85 compiler is the "flagship" COBOL compiler for that
system, it continues to be the target of significant enhancements, it is
*heavily* used in production, its customer base is growing, other products
(e.g., LINC aka EAE) are dependent on it.  And it's written in Pascal83.

The COBOL74 compiler for that system is written in an ALGOL dialect (NEWP;
it was converted from ALGOL itself about ten years ago), and is also heavily
used, though it no longer benefits from significant enhancements.   The only
language compilers that I'm aware of that were once used in production on
Burroughs Large Systems but are no longer supported are Basic, FORTRAN (66),
PL/I, APL/B, ESPOL (another ALGOL derivative replaced by NEWP) and
COBOL(68).  No dialect of Pascal is numbered among them.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
other languages and not ones that mean something quite different.

Proposed COBOL Pascal Python PERL  VB   C/C++
-------- ----- ------ ------ ----  ---  -----
    ^     AND    and    and   and  and  &&
    |     OR     or     or    or   or   ||
    !     NOT    not    not   not  not  !

familiar with what Cobol already offers, contrary to the assertions
made.  But then RW probably didn't know this.

And which languages use the proposed symbols:

Proposed       Pascal Python   PERL    C/C++
-------- ----- ------ ------   ----    ------
    ^          deref  bit xor  xor     bit xor
    |            -    bit or   bit or  bit or
    !            -     -       not     not

So, far from providing something that programmers in other languages
are used to, this is for symbols that are to mean something quite
different from their use elsewhere.  That'll make them 'switch gears'.
```

```
Richard wrote:

> OK, own up, which joker faked this up to make RW look like a complete
> idiot ?

You are just plain bloody cruel Richard. I couldn't help but giggle. I don't
give a belly laugh - just giggle. If I thought it would help Robert, I would
own up as the joker.

Your fault Bill - you challenged him - and he submitted.

Robert - twice I've seen you refer to the 17 years from COBOL '85 to COBOL
2002. With a bit of Anderson type accounting you can reduce that to 5 years if
you accept COBOL '97 which gave the intrinsic functions.

Anyway reference the 'delay'. There were two factors :-

- Bill can expand if necessary. Over time as they came to introduce new
features for 2002 they found that to fit in, (backwards compatibility), they
had to consider some of the COBOL '85 wording which was somewhat ambiguous in
definition. In introducing the 'new', revamp the 'old' so the former could fit.
A pretty dull, time consuming exercise

- Sticking with our 17 year time frame they were OVERWHELMED with suggested
features - how many I don't know. Nor do I  know the date, but they had to
stop, take stock and start prioritizing, (a) Do this now (b) Kind of
interesting and (c) Put on the back burner. There's no way of knowing, but
possibly a sizeable chunk might have been in the category that I would classify
'nit picking', as opposed to new, real solid and *significant* features.

Guess what - some, just  like your current suggestion were probably 'nit
pickers'. Small ditsy features which they accommodated. But firiggin OO was put
into either (b) or (c) above, as a result !

Accepting the confusion over symbols that Chuck and Richard have pointed out, I
don't really give a damn whether they are introduced or not, just so, that
along with the symbol for in-line invocation, I don't damn well have to use
them.

! instead of say AND - 1 character instead of 3
# instead of say OR - 1 character instead of 2

BIG DEAL !

Now the only symbol I'm interested in is the <>. Been using it so long, didn't
even realize it wasn't yet in the Standard until Bill pointed that out. With a
non mathematical mind I can see the logic of <>, it isn't < and it isn't >, so
<> doesn't require too much brainwork.

And as for your assertion "unanimous" -  I got really bored reading the whole
thread(s) leading up to this, and seeing, rough guess, some half dozen folks
discussed this with you, and even assuming they agreed with you, hardly
constitutes unanimity.

Consider: at the end of the day, will introduction of your symbols truly
improve COBOL ? And to me more importantly, don't let's have another nit picker
on the list which will delay discussion on OO Standard Classes or XML  - wish
they would co-opt;  9 people, (J4),  can only stretch themselves so far.

Jimmy
```

```
Currently SQL WHERE conditions have a nice fit with Cobol (except
unequal of course but that will be fixed with '<>').

Or are these non-superficial considerations beyond you ?
```

```
I have a lot of sympathy with this and I can understand your frustration.

The fact is, I, for one, would have gladly volunteered if I was satisfied
there was no profit involved.

I was not then (and am STILL not) satisfied that this "non-profit"
organization can sell the report for around $200 a copy, charge people (and,
especially, Corporations), for the dubious privelege of sitting on the
committee, and then EXPECT that lovers of COBOL should volunteer their time
and expertise for NOTHING. I would have done as much as they wanted if they
paid me.

I make a living by using the couple of pounds of grey slush that floats
around in my head. It has value. I am not persuaded by the "prestige" of
joining an exclusive club, as some of the members obviously were...

I work free for Charities of my own choosing, and they are Organizations
that I am satisfied need some help and support, and can't afford to pay the
going rate. ANSI/ISO/J4 doesn't come anywhere near that category. Hence, I
didn't get involved.

Obviously, I can't speak for others, but the above is an honest statement of
the reasons leading to one person's failure to get involved. (It may also
help to explain why my position was so vehement when I saw the lack of
achievement that came out of it...).

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-06-03T02:42:10+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3edbe6e0.357621858@news.optonline.net>`

```
Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>Robert Wagner wrote:
>
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
>for unequal.

You argue on the one hand that comparison operators are not logic, then on the
other hand the LOGICAL truth of a statement can (or should) be negated by
reversing the comparison operators. You are contradicting yourself. 

When I argued the logical operators are the ones that should be changed, you
said that's not what you are used to. 

Conclusion: you are used to illogic. 

>>>A change to having '<>' or '!"' as an inequality comparator has noting
>>>to do with having a symbol for 'not'.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>logical not ? and how did you extrapolate that to '^' and '|' ?

Your note said "I also do = to !=". It did not say 'I also do = to <>'.

If ! is read not in !=, some would think ! meant not elsewhere. That's why C and
Python both use ! for two purposes. Seems reasonable to me, especially in COBOL,
where it isn't made clear that 'not =' is a single operator. 

If we're going to create a symbol for not, in the interest of regularity we
should also have symbols for and/orl.

The 7-bit national replacement characters apply to 7-bit terminals, not
computers. All modern computers are 8 bit or better. Perhaps users in some
technical backwater are still using 7-bit terminals, but I think it implausible
anyone is still reading or writing source code on them. 

Worst case, they would revert to the spelled out words we have today.
```

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >No it doesn't.  '!=' and '<>' are comparison operators, not logic.  Python, 
> >VB, Pascal derivitives (and many other languages) use and, or, not for 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> You argue on the one hand that comparison operators are not logic, 

"Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote 
>
> As I see it in COBOL, there are SIX classes of operators in '02-standard
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> (f) Logical (AND, OR and NOT).

Note groups (e) and (f).

> then on the
> other hand the LOGICAL truth of a statement can (or should) be negated by
> reversing the comparison operators. You are contradicting yourself. 

The logical truth of two statements may be opposite.  How those two
statements are contructed is irrelevant.  It happens that if the two
statements have different comparison operators then their logical
truth may be opposite.

There is _nothing_ to contradict.  I don't _have_ to arrive at the 2nd
statement by doing something specific, I can do it anyway that _I_
please, there is nothing 'magic' that means that one incantation works
and another doesn't.

In a similar vein:

   '<>'    ->  unequal
   '!='    ->  unequal
   'NOT =' -> unequal

In Cobol NOT is found as part of the syntax of many verbs and
conditional expressions.  For example:

        READ ...
            AT END imperitive statement
            NOT AT END inperitive statement
        END-READ

In this context NOT is part of the syntax of the READ statement and is
_NOT_ a logical operator.

In many condition statements NOT forms part of the syntax - which is
why 'NOT >=' is invalid, it is not part of the syntax of the >=
condition.

  'a NOT = b' is 'a unequal b' - a simple relation condition

  this is not the same as 'NOT ( a = b )' a negated simple condition.

> When I argued the logical operators are the ones that should be changed, you
> said that's not what you are used to. 

I am certainly not used to ^|! for anything that you proposed for
them.

> Conclusion: you are used to illogic. 

You manage to draw silly conclusions about many things that you don't
understand.

> Your note said "I also do = to !=". It did not say 'I also do = to <>'.

So ?  I don't have a problem with either.  Take your hang ups
elsewhere.

> If ! is read not in !=, some would think ! meant not elsewhere. That's why C
> and > Python both use ! for two purposes. 

You are _way_ beyond your actual knowledge.  Python uses 'not' (as do
many others).  In both C and Python (and many others) '!=' is a single
operator that means 'unequal'.

> Seems reasonable to me, especially
> in COBOL, where it isn't made clear that 'not =' is a single operator. 

Well it is, the NOT is part of the syntax of '='.  I doubt that being
'clear to you' is in any way significant.
 
> If we're going to create a symbol for not, 

Why would we create a 'symbol for not' ?  Unequal is '<>'.  I like
'not'.

> in the interest of regularity we should also have symbols for and/orl. 

Given that I see no point in a symbol for 'not' then I propose we
maintain the existing regularity by leaving 'and' and 'or' alone.

> >Consistency is not a strong point with you is it.
> 
> As I showed above -- using logic rather than name calling -- you are
> inconsistent. 

Except you didn't, you merely contrived some stretch based on what ? -
not realising that NOT is part of the syntax of '='.

> Worst case, they would revert to the spelled out words we have today.

Best case: we all continue to use the nice set we are all used to. If
that includes spelling out GREATER THAN then that is OK too.
 
> That's why I'll propose the C/Java symbols. 

And those are still foreign to Cobol, SQL, VB, Python, PERL, and a
host of other  languages.

> Programmers comfortable with those languages can use words. 

So, you don't care if they are the 'next programmer' for your code ?
 
> >However, in general, C++ and Java programmers do not want to use Cobol and

> It's just speculation. 

As usual, yet elsewhere you claim to use 'facts'.
 
> Bit-wise operators (written in function form) aren't used by 'average'
> programmers.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 
>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>and another doesn't.

Your method works only on relationals. A more generic approach works on all
conditionals. Of instance:

(not a or b) and (c or not d) 

negates to 

(a or not b) or (not c and d)

If b happens to be a relational such as 'x=y', you could write 'not b' as
'x<>y'. But if b is an 88 or user-defined function, it must be written 'not b'.
My way -- 'x not = y' is visually closer to the generic solution. 

Yes, I realize 'not =' is technically a single condition equivalent to <>. But
it's two COBOL words. 

>In Cobol NOT is found as part of the syntax of many verbs and
>conditional expressions.  For example:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>_NOT_ a logical operator.

We all know COBOL is context-sensitive. What does that prove in re NOT as a
logical operator?

>In many condition statements NOT forms part of the syntax - which is
>why 'NOT >=' is invalid, it is not part of the syntax of the >=
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>  this is not the same as 'NOT ( a = b )' a negated simple condition.

The COBOL standard doesn't make that very clear. It says you can put NOT in
front of =, but fails to make a distinction between that use of NOT and a
logical operator. On the contrary, it suggests they are the same when it forbids
the double negative NOT NOT. You cannot say 'if not not a' (logical operator)
nor can you say 'if x = y and not not > z'. Why? If the second NOT is part of a
relational, as you say, the statement should be interpreted 'if x = y and not <=
z'. If the second compiles -- it does under Micro Focus -- then the first should
compile as well. They are logically the same. In this case, COBOL is treating
the second NOT as though it is a logical operator.
```

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

>If you want to change NOT to <symbol de jour> then it will be
>confusing if sometimes it can be a symbol and sometimes it must be
>NOT.  Your proposal only changed the logical operators and not the
>syntax of the the various statemnets, and also did not change how SQL
>must be written.

>While claiming that you want to make things _consistent_ your proposal
>is _inconsistent_ because NOT will still be compulsory in places.

I'd be happy with making ! and NOT synonymous everywhere, just like THRU and
THROUGH. Why is this a major problem?

>> The COBOL standard doesn't make that very clear. It says you can put NOT in
>> front of =, but fails to make a distinction between that use of NOT and a
>> logical operator. 

Correcting myself -- the quote below shows the standard DOES make a distinction,
but it is not in the syntax rules for relational operators, where one would
expect to find it.

>> On the contrary, it suggests they are the same when it forbids
>> the double negative NOT NOT. You cannot say 'if not not a' (logical operator)
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>Is perfectly valid, but not useful.

Nope. See below.

>> nor can you say 'if x = y and not not > z'. Why? If the second NOT is part of
>> a > relational, as you say, the statement should be interpreted 'if x = y and
>> not <=  z'. 
>
>Can you show where this is not valid ?  

From the 2002 standard (85 standard contains similar):

8.6.4.2.3
Table 6   Combinations of conditions, logical operators, and parentheses
NOTE The element pair 'OR NOT' is permissible while the pair 'NOT OR' is not
permissible; the pair 'NOT (' is permissible while the pair 'NOT NOT' is not
permissible

Also:

8.8.4.2.4.2 Syntax Rules for Abbreviated combined relation conditions

2) The result of implied insertion shall comply with the rules of table 5,
Combinations of conditions, logical
operators, and parentheses. [They meant to say table 6]
3) The word NOT shall not be followed immediately by the word NOT or the words
IS NOT.

>> If the second compiles -- it does under Micro Focus -- then the first should
>> compile as well.
>
>It does.  Not only that the manual explains it specifically.

It doesn't compile. Micro Focus rejects NOT NOT as an error. 
 
>> They are logically the same. In this case, COBOL is treating
>> the second NOT as though it is a logical operator.
>
>Read (from MF manual) for abbreviated conditions: "" If NOT is
>immediately followed by GREATER, >, LESS, < ...""

The standard says:

b) If the relational operator following the word NOT is a simple relational
operator, then the NOT participates as part of the simple relational operator; 
[if that were true,  'x = y and not not > z' would compile.]

otherwise,

c) The NOT is interpreted as a logical operator and, therefore, the implied
insertion of subject or relational operator results in a negated relation
condition.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0306092146.75ef6459@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <217e491a.0305311134.7dccfc1d@posting.google.com> <3eda4e4d.253011869@news.optonline.net> <bbe1mj$hjc$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3edbe6e0.357621858@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306022344.24fe26aa@posting.google.com> <3ee20970.109770318@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306072222.74be4070@posting.google.com> <3ee37364.202444547@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306082006.6b934af4@posting.google.com> <3ee513f1.88498649@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >> >Yes you can.  You can have as many 'negated simple conditions' as you
> >> >want.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> Changing your premises or 'clarifying'?  When I do that, I'm crucified. 

You _can_ have as many 'negated simple conditions' as you want.

Standards compliance would require the parentheses, but MF (on my
machine) _does_ compile it without.

> You guessed NOT NOT would work and you were simply WRONG. There is no "MAY
> need to put parentheses", they are required by the standard. 

Actually it _did_ work.  You may need to put parenthese depending on
compiler od settings of standards compliance flags.

Of course your use of pointers which depends on non-standard features
also may need changing depending on compiler or settings of standards
compliance.

> >Howver 'NOT NOT <=' would not as the syntax for <= does not allow 'IS
> >NOT <='.
> 
> The 85 standard is ambiguous. In simple relationals,  NOT <= is verboten. But
> in Abbreviated compound conditionals, NOT (anything) is permissible. 

It is not ambiguous at all.  There is no optional [NOT] in the stntax
of 'IS <=', while there is an optional 'NOT' in the syntax of 'IS
[NOT] <'.

In abbreviated conditions it quite clearly states how NOT is
interpreted.

> What I see here is people having trouble with logic, just like Joe in the next
> cubicle .. trying to cope with English language skills. 

What I see here is somebody having trouble with Cobol.

> That's why nobody supports my proposal for symbols.

First of all I am pleased to see that you have changed your claim of
'unanimous support'.  But now it is 'nobody'.  If is ain't white it
must be black ?

Your proposals are not well thought through in any way.  Half-baked is
a fair commentary on them.

Your proposal is for 'logical operators', it does not cover the
relational operators and the use of optional NOT in these.  It also
makes no mention of the syntax of many statements where NOT is used,
such as NOT AT END.

With your proposal one may say:

         IF ! A = 1

But may not say:

         IF A ! = 1  
or:      IF A != 1

The NOT is question is part of the syntax of the 'IS [NOT] =' relation
condition and not a logical operator.

In an abbreviated condition this may result in having to use NOT
rather than !:

       IF A = 1 OR NOT < 6 OR = 3      

  ie   IF ( A = 1 ) OR ( A IS NOT < 6 ) OR ( A = 3 )

If, however it was written with ! this would change its meaning:

       IF A = 1 OR ! < 6 OR = 3

Your new proposal is even worse because it doesn't specify who should
choose the characters to use:

       J4 ?
       Vendor ?
       Programmer ?

If the proposal is for all of these then it will be the programmer
that can choose to use '^' or '/\' or '&&' for AND.  Can they use any
and all of these in one program ?

If it is for J4 to choose and they choose, say, '/\' and '|' and 'ï¿½'
then how will this be 'welcomed by C/Java programmers' ?  Or by anyone
else ?

Half-baked is a generous appraisal.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-08T22:33:25-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bc0v66$jal$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ed850da.122572452@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0305311134.7dccfc1d@posting.google.com> <3eda4e4d.253011869@news.optonline.net> <bbe1mj$hjc$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3edbe6e0.357621858@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306022344.24fe26aa@posting.google.com> <3ee20970.109770318@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0306072222.74be4070@posting.google.com> <217e491a.0306081241.66ef127a@posting.google.com>`

```
In case anyone questions Richard's statement, the '85 Standard, page VI-60,
in the section, "6.3.2.3 Precedence of Logical Operators and the Use of
Parentheses" clearly indicates that a "NOT" may *not* be followed
immediately by another "NOT" in a valid (conforming) statement.  In fact,
immediately after "Table 1: Combinations of Conditions, Logical Operators,
and Parentheses" is the explicit statement,

"Thus, the element pair 'OR NOT' is permissible while the pair 'NOT OR' is
not lermissible; the pair 'NOT (' is permissible while the pair 'NOT NOT' is
not ,ermissible."
```

```
    ***

Some examples that you might want to look at from various threads of what
was ACTUALLY said are:

"Certainly, I know that there are programming languages with a "symbol" for
the logical "NOT"  "AND" and "OR" - so providing such to COBOL wouldn't be
totally awful."

"COBOL actually has a reasonably complete set of arithmetic operators
represented by symbols. It has a complete set of equality/inequality
operators represented by symbols. Only the three Boolean operators are
represented by words. All their spelled-out words are very short and perhaps
therefore do not so much call out for symbols as some other operators.

But I can't understand the logic that because COBOL doesn't use symbols for
all operators, I should therefore as much as possible not use symbols for
any of them, even though I find expressions with symbols clearer. The more
logical approach is to take what I can get."

"Mr Wagner, please be so kind as to demonstrate how one's ability to readily
recognise a set of symbols has anything, whatsoever, to do with logical
capabilities... at your leisure, of course."

"Asymmetry is a problem with the "language".  You haven't shown that one of
the two is a "complete" set and the other is an "incomplete set".  However,
even if you had, I don't think that would (necessarily) mean that simply not
being a "complete" set means that it is "less good" to use one or the  other
option - where both exists."

"> You don't get it. Yes, COBOL has symbols for math operations but not for
> logical  operations.

Either way, it like every other system I can name is "incomplete", by your
standards.

So the answer is, yes, I would use such an incomplete system."

"> Not the important logical operators: AND, OR and NOT. Suppose math used
symbols

> notation.

Only until I got used to it <shrug>.  In fact if that is what they did do
then I _would_ be used to it.  In fact they _do_ use sine, cosine, tan, log
etc which _are_ words and it _doesn't_ displease me."
```

```
The only constant statement about the group's views is that whichever side I'm

Referencing the views of such a mercurial group only weakens my suggestion, so
I'll take your advice. Here is the email I'm planning to send to the J4 chair in
a few days:

-----------------------------------------------------------
During heated discussion in comp.lang.cobol (CLC), some participants said I had
misrepresented their views in my Enhancement Request re logical symbols. As a
result, I'm submitting the below as a complete replacement. The suggestion
itself has not changed except for the addition of alternative symbols. The only
change is deleting reference to CLC in the rationale. 

Revised version follows:

I suggest adding symbols for the logical operators:

AND  ^ or &&
OR    | or || (ASCII 7C, EBCDIC 5F) 
NOT  !

I feel these symbols would especially welcomed by younger programmers coming
from other (programming) language cultures.

I also favor '<>', which I understand is already under consideration.

Regards, 
Robert

The context of that discussion was logic, not symbols vs. words. Specifically,

(supplied) a LOGICAL operator rather than a RELATIONAL operator. The distinction
was what kind of operator should be modified. 

>"Asymmetry is a problem with the "language".  You haven't shown that one of
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols"

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-06-01T15:56:30-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bbdpa1$r59$1@slb2.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ed6bba0.18818906@news.optonline.net> <3ed72645_4@news.athenanews.com> <3ed86769.128348745@news.optonline.net> <bbbec4$abl$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net> <3eda2ea1.244902095@news.optonline.net>`

```
The ACCURATE (as far as I can tell) "consensus" (not unanimous) view within
this group is that symbols vs words is a STYLE issue.  Such issues do *NOT*
have a "superior" option (i.e. NEITHER is superior to the other as this is a
STYLE issue).

Some of us (possibly I am unique on this - but won't swear to it either way)
think that it would NOT be a "bad" think to have a complete set of both
symbols and words - to provide a "full set of options" for either STYLE.
HOWEVER, (as I have already communicated to J4), I view this in the "nice to
have" not in the "very important" category.  The current repertoire of
features for both relational and logical operators is sufficient and I do
*NOT* think it is worth significant resources to "add" more (either words or
symbols.

In addition (as also sent to J4), I see "reasonable" choices for "NOT" and
"OR" - but (so far) haven't seen ANY really good choice of a symbol for
"AND".

FYI, the following is exactly what I sent to J4

" ... I think adding a "complete set of symbols" for logical operator would
be a "NICE TO HAVE". I do NOT think it is worth a "lot of time and
resources" (much less contentious debate) - but do think it might be useful.
I WOULD like to see a future COBOL Standard include support for

<> to mean NOT EQUAL (as originally specified in the CCC JOD)

If I were to "suggest" symbols (from the ISO 8859 "Latin-1" - which I think
is a reasonable assumption), then I would probably go with

^ (X'5E') or � (X'AC') - for NOT

| (X'7C') or � (X'A6') - for OR

I do think, however, there is a "problem" with what you use for AND. It
seems to me that the most "logical" would (of course) be "&" - but I believe
that would be impossible to use as BOTH a logical operator and (as currently
defined) the concatenation operator - because of code such as:

If A not = "B" & "C"

(is it a concatenation operator or is this a combined abbreviated
condition?)

If there were GENERAL consensus that adding a complete set of logical
operators represented as SYMBOLS was a GOOD IDEA, then we could discuss what
would be a possible symbol for "AND" (from within 8859-1). "@" (X'3E') might
be a possibility. Others might be "#" "!" "�" or "�" - none of these are
really good, but are possible. (I would be interested in knowing if any of
this "plain text" note causes problems to the non-US recipients)"
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message

> There is a paper for review by J4 called,
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…

I didn't follow much of this discussion as I found it tedious in the
extreme. However, looking at Robert's succinct summary I find it hard to
believe that the "unanimous" or even "majority" opinion was in favour of
symbols for Logical operators.

My first impulse was to write to Don Schricker and say that I, for one,
don't see the need to offer alternatives to AND OR and NOT.

But then I thought about it some more and decided that, as long as no one
FORCES the replacement of the words with these symbols, there is no harm
done.

It is unlikely we'll see it implemented anyway, if past experience is
anything to go on.

Pete.
```

```
On Sat, 31 May 2003 14:13:17 GMT, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
wrote:

>
>You say you need more disk space?
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Looks to me like you have plenty of space. 

That is assuming the command "rm" works.

A good programmer knows better than just to issue an "rm -r *", as it
is bound to fail.

Instead he/she uses
find . -follow -depth -exec rm {} \;

or
find . -follow -depth -print|xargs rm

This is a free lesson. Take it while it lasts.
Frederico Fonseca
ema il: frederico_fonseca at syssoft-int.com
```

#### ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols" <- Bad idea

- **From:** "Anon" <anon@anon.org>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T22:36:18-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bb97vl$odf$1@ngspool-d02.news.aol.com>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
I am just a COBOL applications programmer. I've been coding
COBOL for over 20 years. I don't sit on any committees, I
don't write books or articles. I have any pie-in-sky
academic credentials. I do have a reputation for writing
stable, efficient code. I've mentored any number of younger
folks on the job and thing I stress over and over is
"Clarity not Cleverness" or another way "Don't be Clever at
the expense of Clarity". Cutesy code has no place in a
business application. The beauty of COBOL and what has
always been it's hallmark is YOU CAN READ IT! It's English
like "self documenting" style is what sets it apart and
what it makes the best language for business logic.
The best code is the clearest code.
The standard symbols + - = / * > <  are fine.
It matters not to me whether you say "is not equal to" or
whether you say "not =", both are equally clear.
Even those who don't know programming can READ it.
But IMO, "is ! =" or "^=' is NOT CLEAR.

use: AND, OR, NOT, FROM, UNTIL and so on. Spell it out, it's COBOL.

What's next then: "perform varying C ; 1 by ++ ; C > 10"? All those
hieroglyphics just cause mass confusion.

COBOL does not need a bunch c/c++ like CRYPTOGRAPHY!
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: J4 paper on "symbols" <- Bad idea

- **From:** "Warren Simmons" <wsimmons5@cox.net>
- **Date:** 2003-05-30T23:28:41-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<y_XBa.56023$%42.19662@fed1read06>`
- **References:** `<bb63av$e7v$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <bb97vl$odf$1@ngspool-d02.news.aol.com>`

```
Great, someone else said it before I did.

As I see it, a pre-processor might offer an option to help
bridge some geeks by translating symbols to words, but
generally, I don't even like to see sample code that reverts to
using something like:

        02 zilch pic x(nn).

There are times when the word I'd like to use does not quickly
come to mind. However, the guy who paid me would not tolerate
such short cuts.  In our NY Headquarters prior to the existance
of a compiler for the 1400 series, we insisted that the conversion
be to COBOL using predefined statements with the autocoder
equivalent.  They helped cross train between COBOL and assembler
also. The result was we had two source listing until we got the compiler.
Everyone involved could understand the source.

Warren Simmons
wsimmons5@cox.net
```
